مخالف في انتهائِهِ لنموّ الأُذُنِ ، وكذا نمو الأنف والرَّجُلِ وغيرِهما مختلف ، بل أصابع اليد المتحدةُ المحلَّ وأصابعُ الرَّجُلِ وأسنانُ الفم مختلفة في نموها ، وترى بعض الأعضاء نموها في الطولِ أَكثرُ مِنَ العرض ، وبعضها بالعكس ... إلى غير ذلكَ مِنْ صفاتِ اختلافِ النمو ، وكلٌّ على أبلغ ما يكونُ مِنَ المناسبة لمصلحته الخاصة به . أفيرضى عاقل أن يسند هذا الصنعَ العجيب والشكل الغريب لشيء مِنَ العالم منفرداً أو مجتمعاً ، فضلاً أنْ يسندَهُ إِلى خصوصيةِ مَوَاتٍ لا يسمع ولا يبصر ولا يغني شيئاً ؟! كلا والله ، إِنَّما يليقُ أنْ يفعلَهُ مَنْ ليس كمثلِهِ شَيء ، مالك الملك ، المحيط علمه بكلِّ شيءِ ، الذي لا يتعاصى على قدرته التامَّةِ وإرادتِهِ النافذة شيءٌ مِنَ الكائناتِ ، فتبارك الله أحسنُ الخالقينَ وللطبائعيين هنا تقريرات وهوس يُمَج ذكرُهُ وتسويدُ الصُّحُفِ بهِ ، وهدْمُ أساساتهم الواهية مستبين لكلِّ موفّق ، والاطلاع على مذاهبهم يدلُّ على عظيم ما ابتلوا به والعياذُ باللهِ مِنْ سَلْبِ العقل والإيمانِ ، والاتصاف بصفاتِ المجانين والبُلْهِ والصبيانِ نسأله سبحانَهُ أنْ يمنَّ علينا بحسن المعرفة ، ويختم لنا بأشرف الخواتم عند مماتِنا ، ويحفظنا مِنَ البدع ظاهراً وباطناً في جميع حالاتنا وأوقاتِنا ، فإنَّهُ لا حول ولا قُوَّةَ إلا به ، والصلاة والسلام على سيدنا ومولانا محمد العروة الوثقى ، والعصمة الكبرى لِمَنْ تمسَّكَ بنَتِهِ وسنة الراشدين مِنْ آلِهِ وصحبه
Different in its ending for the growth of the ear, and similarly the growth of the nose and the leg and others is different; rather the fingers of the hand—united in location—and the fingers of the foot and the teeth of the mouth are different in their growth. And you see some organs growing more in length than width, and some the opposite... and other characteristics of difference in growth, and each is in the most perfect proportion for its particular benefit. Would a rational person be pleased to attribute this wondrous craftsmanship and strange form to something from the world alone or collectively, let alone attribute it to the particularity of dead matter that neither hears nor sees nor avails anything? No, by Allah, it is only befitting that He does it who is unlike anything, the Owner of dominion, whose knowledge encompasses everything, whose perfect power and penetrating will nothing in existence resists. So blessed be Allah, the best of creators. And for the naturalists here are absurdities and madness whose mention is repulsive and blackening pages with it. The demolition of their weak foundations is clear for every successful person. Examining their doctrines indicates the great affliction they have been tried with—Allah forbid—from deprivation of reason and faith, and being characterized with the qualities of the insane, the imbecilic, and children. We ask Him, exalted, to bestow upon us good knowledge, and to seal for us with the noblest ends at our death, and to preserve us from innovations outwardly and inwardly in all our states and times. Truly there is no power and no strength except with Him. And blessings and peace be upon our master Muhammad, the firmest handhold and the greatest infallibility for whoever clings to his prophethood and the sunnah of the rightly-guided from his family and companions.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is a teleological argument from design for the existence of a wise Creator. The author first observes the diversity and proportion in the growth of human body parts: ears, nose, legs, fingers, teeth—all differ in the manner and timing of growth, and each is perfectly suited to its function. The argument then poses a rhetorical question: would any rational person attribute this remarkable design to mere blind material forces, whether acting alone or together? Even more absurd would be to ascribe it to inanimate matter that lacks perception and power — the phrase 'dead matter that neither hears nor sees nor avails anything' emphasizes its impotence. The author asserts that such craftsmanship can only emanate from a Being who is absolutely unique, the Owner of dominion, whose knowledge encompasses all, and whose will no existent can resist. He then launches a sharp refutation of the naturalists (the materialists), dismissing their ideas as madness and moral corruption. The passage concludes with a prayer for sound knowledge, a good end, and protection from innovations, followed by salutations upon the Prophet and his family and companions. The argument is complete: from design to Designer, then polemic against alternative explanations, then ratification through praise and prayer.

Argument from Contingency of the Self
Sanusi proves the temporal origination of the world by analogy with the human self.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses the analogy of the human self to demonstrate the temporal origination of the entire cosmos, relying on the principle of the equality of similars.

Core Idea
Your own contingency proves the world's creation.

This passage presents a powerful proof from analogy that is both personal and universal. Sanusi invites you to reflect on your own existence—your origin from a sperm drop, your contingency—and then argues that everything in the world shares this nature. Because you are a temporally originated being, and the world is like you in every relevant respect, the world too must be created. This argument anchors the entire kalam proof for God's existence in immediate self-awareness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the repeated phrase 'like you' (mithluka) which signals the analogy.
2
Identify key components
Find the key elements: the sperm drop (al-nutfah), the principle of equality of similars, the specifier (mukhassis), and the conclusion about the whole world.
3
Trace the logical steps
Follow the argument: from your own contingency to the world's contingency, then to the necessity of a specifier, and finally to the impossibility of any part being eternal.
Pay attention to the hypothetical objection and how the principle blocks it.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that the entire world is temporally originated and dependent on a free agent. Consider the implication that all things glorify Him.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومِنْ هنا أيضاً تعلمُ : أنَّ تلك النطفة وسائر العالم لم يكنْ ثم كانَ ؛ إذْ كلُّهُ مثلُكَ ؛ جِرْمٌ يعمرُ فراغاً ، يمكنُ وجودُهُ وعدمه ، واتصافه بما هو عليهِ مِنَ المقادير والصفاتِ المخصوصة وبغيرها ، فيحتاج كما احتجت إلى مُخصص يُخصِّصُهُ بما هو عليهِ ؛ لوجوب استواء المثلين في كل ما يجبُ ويجوز ويستحيلُ ، وقد وجبَ لذاتِكَ سبقُ العدم، فكذلك يجبُ لسائر العالم المماثل لكَ ؛ إذ لو جاز أنْ يكونَ بعضُ العالم قديماً ، والقِدَمُ لا يكون إلا واجباً للقديم ؛ لِمَا يأتي.. لَلَزِمَ أنْ يختص أحدُ المِثْلين عن مِثْلِهِ بصفة واجبةٍ ، وهو محالٌ ؛ لِمَا يلزمُ مِنِ اجتماعِ متنافيين وهو أن يكونَ مِثْلاً غيرَ مِثْل ! فخرج لكَ بالنظرِ في ذاتِكَ ، وانعقاد التماثل بينك وبينَ سائر الممكنات .. البرهان القاطع على حدوثِ العالم كله ؛ علوهِ وسفلِهِ ، عرشِهِ وكرسيه ، أصله وفرعِهِ ، وأنَّ جميعَهُ عاجز عن إيجادِ نفسِهِ وعن إيجادِ غيرِهِ كعجزِكَ ، وأَنَّ الجميع مفتقر إلى فاعل مختار كافتقارِكَ ، وإِنْ مِنْ شيء إلا يُسبِّحُ بحمدِهِ
And from here also you know: that sperm drop and the rest of the world did not exist then came to be; since all of it is like you: a body occupying a space, its existence and non-existence are possible, and its being qualified with the quantities and specific attributes it has and with others. So it needs, as you needed, a specifier (mukhassis) to specify it as it is; because of the necessity of the equality of two similar things in everything that is necessary, possible, and impossible. And since prior non-existence was necessitated (wajaba) for your essence, so likewise it is necessitated for the entire world which is similar to you; because if it were possible that some part of the world be eternal, and eternity is not except necessary for the eternal, as follows... then one of two similar things would be distinguished from its similar by a necessary attribute, and that is impossible because of the resulting composition of two contradictories: that it be similar and not similar. So there emerges for you, by reflection on your own essence and the establishment of similarity between you and all other possibles, the conclusive proof for the temporal origination (huduth) of the whole world: its height and depth, its Throne and Kursi, its root and branch; and that all of it is incapable of bringing itself into existence and of bringing anything else into existence, just as you are incapable; and that all is in need of a free agent (fa'il mukhtar), just as you are in need. And there is not a thing but glorifies His praise.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the temporal origination (huduth) of the entire world based on analogy with the human self. The argument proceeds as follows: (1) The sperm drop (i.e., the origin of each human) and indeed the whole world did not exist then came to exist. (2) Everything in the world is like you: it is a body occupying space, its existence and non-existence are merely possible (i.e., contingent). (3) Contingent things require a 'specifier' (mukhassis) to bring them into existence in their specific form. (4) The principle of the equality of two similar things (al-mithlayn) dictates that if two entities are alike in all respects, they must be alike in what is necessary, possible, and impossible for them. (5) Since you (the reader) know that you were preceded by non-existence (you were created), and the world is similar to you in being contingent body, it must also be preceded by non-existence. (6) If some part of the world were eternal (qadim), eternity is a necessary attribute, so that part would be distinguished from its similar (another contingent) by a necessary attribute, which contradicts the equality of similars. Therefore, the whole world—every part, from the highest to the lowest, from the Throne to the footstool—is temporally originated (hadith). (7) Consequently, all of it is incapable of creating itself or anything else, and is entirely dependent on a free agent (fā‘il mukhtār). The argument is a classic kalam proof from contingency, here using the self as the primary example of a contingent being. The conclusion is that the entire cosmos is created and dependent on a Creator.

Key Terms
النطفة
تماثل
مخصص
حدوث العالم
فاعل مختار
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument merely points to the self's creation and concludes the world's creation without further reasoning.
Correction: The argument relies on the rational principle of the equality of similars to extend the contingency of the self to all possible beings. It is not a simple analogy but a logical deduction based on shared contingency.

Checkpoint
What is the key principle that allows the author to extend the proof from the contingency of the self to the entire world?
A) The principle of causality
B) The principle of the equality of two similar things in all possible and necessary judgments
C) The principle of sufficient reason
D) The principle of non-contradiction

Key Takeaway
The entire cosmos, like your own self, is temporally originated and depends on a free Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi says that by reflecting on your own essence, a conclusive proof for the world's creation emerges. Have you ever truly reflected on your own existence—the fact that you did not exist and then came to be—to arrive at certainty about the Creator? What holds you back from doing so daily?
?
The passage declares that you are incapable of bringing yourself into existence, just like the rest of creation. Yet we often live as if we are self-sufficient. When you consider this, what is the root of that self-sufficiency in your heart, and how can you actively cultivate a constant sense of dependence on the Free Agent?
?
If the entire cosmos, from the Throne to the microbe, is similar to you in contingency and need for a Specifier, why do you still treat some aspects of your life as if they are under your control? What would change if you truly believed that every moment you exist is sustained by a free act of the Creator?

Allah's Existence
beginner
5 min
The Analogy of Self-Reflection
Sanusi introduces the analogy between human creation and the universe.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the foundational analogy Sanusi uses: just as you were created from a drop, so must the entire world be created, because of the principle that two likes share the same modal status.

Core Idea
You and the world are alike; what is true of you is true of the world.

Sanusi begins a powerful proof by directing you to reflect on your own existence. Your body, formed from a drop, once did not exist. Now, he asks you to see that the entire world is like you—a body occupying space, contingent. This simple observation, when combined with a logical principle, becomes a decisive argument for the world's creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the analogy
Read the Arabic and translation, noting that Sanusi compares the drop (nutfah) and the world to you.
The analogy is not just similarity but exact likeness in being a body occupying space.
2
Understand the principle of equality of likes
Sanusi states that two likes must be equal in what is necessary, possible, and impossible. This is a key logical principle.
3
Apply the principle to yourself and the world
Since non-existence preceded your essence, and the world is like you, non-existence must also precede the world. Therefore, the world is created.
This leads to the need for a determiner (mukhaṣṣiṣ).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومِنْ هنا أيضاً تعلمُ : أنَّ تلك النطفة وسائر العالم لم یكنْ ثم كانَ ؛ إذْ كلُّهُ مثلُكَ ؛ جِرْمٌ يعمرُ فراغاً ، يمكنُ وجودُهُ وعدمه ، واتصافه بما هو عليهِ مِنَ المقادير والصفاتِ المخصوصة وبغيرها ، فيحتاج كما احتجت إلى مُخصص يُخصِّصُهُ بما هو عليهِ ؛ لوجوب استواء المثلين في كل ما يجبُ ويجوز ويستحيلُ ، وقد وجبَ لذاتِكَ سبقُ العدم، فكذلك يجبُ لسائر العالم المماثل لكَ ؛ إذ لو جاز أنْ يكونَ بعضُ العالم قديماً ، والقِدَمُ لا يكون إلا واجباً للقديم ؛ لِمَا يأتي.. لَلَزِمَ أنْ يختص أحدُ المِثْلين عن مِثْلِهِ بصفة واجبةٍ ، وهو محالٌ ؛ لِمَا يلزمُ مِنِ اجتماعِ متنافيين وهو أن يكونَ مِثْلاً غيرَ مِثْل ! فخرج لكَ بالنظرِ في ذاتِكَ ، وانعقاد التماثل بينك وبينَ سائر الممكنات .. البرهان القاطع على حدوثِ العالم کله ؛ علوهِ وسفلِهِ ، عرشِهِ وكرسيه ، أصله وفرعِهِ ، وأنَّ جميعَهُ عاجز عن إيجادِ نفسِهِ وعن إيجادِ غيرِهِ كعجزِكَ ، وأَنَّ الجميع مفتقر إلى فاعل مختار كافتقارِكَ ، وإِنْ مِنْ شيء إلا يُسبِّحُ بحمدِهِ . حاصلُهُ : أَنَّهُ بعدما استبانَ حدوث الزائدِ مِنَ الذاتِ على النطفة بالضرورة ، وأنَّ النطفة ونحوَها ممَّا يُقدَّرُ مِنَ الطبائع لا أثر لها في شيءٍ مِنَ الذاتِ ، وأنَّ فاعل الذاتِ فاعل مختار . . انعطف هنا بالاستدلالِ بذلكَ الزائدِ مِنَ الذاتِ على حدوثِ تلك النطفة وسائر العالم ، وأنَّ احتياج الجميع إلى فاعل مختار على حد السواء ، ولا أثر لبعض منه في بعض ألبتة . ووجه الاستدلال : تحقُّقُ المماثلة بينَ هذا الزائدِ والعالم كلّهِ ؛ إذْ هذا الزائد أجرام مُتحيّزةً وأعراض قائمة بها ، وسائر العالم كذلكَ والمِثْلانِ يجب استواؤُهما فيما يجبُ ويجوزُ ويستحيل ، وقد وجبَ الحدوث لذلكَ الزائدِ قطعاً ، فكذا يجبُ لسائر العالم ؛ لمماثلتِهِ إيَّاهُ ؛ إذ لو اختلف العالمُ بأن يكونَ بعضُه قديماً وبعضُه حادثاً.. لكان مختلفاً فيما يجب . وبيانُ الملازمةِ : أَنَّ القِدمَ لا يكون إلا واجباً للقديم ، وبرهانه : ما يأتي ؛ مِنْ كونِ القِدَمِ لو كانَ جائزاً للقديم لجاز عليه سبقُ العدمِ ، فيحتاج إلى مُخصِّصِ يُخصِّصُهُ بالوجودِ بدلاً عن العدمِ المجوّز ، وهو نقيضُ القِدَمِ المفروض ، فيلزمُ أنْ يكونَ قديماً غير قديم ! وهو تهافت (۱) ، وهذا معنى قولِهِ : ( والقِدَمُ لا يكون إلا واجباً للقديم لِمَا يأتي ) ، فهو مُعترِضُ بينَ الشرط وجوابه لبيان تلازمهما .
And from here also you learn: that that drop of fluid (nutfah) and the rest of the world were not then came to be; since all of it is like you; a body occupying space, its existence and non-existence possible, and its being characterized by what it has of specific measures and attributes and others, so it needs just as you needed a determiner (mukhaṣṣiṣ) to specify it as it is; because of the necessity of the two likes (mithlān) being equal in everything necessary, possible, and impossible. And non-existence has been preceded for your essence, so likewise it must be for the rest of the world which is similar to you; because if it were possible for some of the world to be eternal (qadīm), and eternity (qidam) is not except necessary for the eternal, as will come, then it would necessitate that one of the two likes be distinguished from its like by a necessary attribute, and that is impossible because it entails the conjunction of two contradictories: that it be a like and not a like! So there emerges for you, by reflecting on your essence and the establishment of likeness (tamāthul) between you and the rest of contingent things, the decisive proof (al-burhān al-qāṭiʿ) for the creation (ḥudūth) of the entire world: its heights and depths, its Throne and Kursī, its principle and branch; and that all of it is incapable of bringing itself into existence and of bringing anything else into existence like your incapacity; and that all of it is in need of a free-acting agent (fāʿil mukhtār) like your need; and there is nothing except that it glorifies His praise. Its summary (ḥāṣiluhu): After it became clear with necessity that the additional part (al-zāʾid) of the essence relative to the drop (al-nutfa) is originated, and that the drop and the like of it from what is considered as natural natures have no effect in anything of the essence, and that the agent of the essence is a free-acting agent, he turns here to infer by means of that additional part of the essence to the origination of that drop and the rest of the world, and that the need of all for a free-acting agent is equal, and there is no effect of some of it in any other at all. And the aspect of inference is: the realization of likeness (al-mumāthala) between this additional part and the entire world; for this additional part is bodies occupying space and accidents inhering in them, and the rest of the world likewise; and the two likes must be equal in what is necessary, possible, and impossible; and origination (al-ḥudūth) has been necessitated for that additional part decisively, so likewise it must be for the rest of the world because of its likeness to it; for if the world differed such that some of it were eternal and some were originated, it would differ in what is necessary. And the explanation of the entailment (mulāzama): that eternity (al-qidam) is only necessary for the eternal, and its proof: what will come; because if eternity were possible for the eternal, then non-existence would be possible for it, so it would need a determiner to specify it with existence instead of the possible non-existence, and that contradicts the supposed eternity, so it would follow that it is eternal and not eternal! And that is incoherence (tahāfut). And this is the meaning of his statement: 'And eternity is only necessary for the eternal, as will come.' So it is an interruption between the condition and its answer to show their entailment.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhān) for the creation (ḥudūth) of the entire universe. The author, Sanusi, constructs an argument by analogy (tamāthul) between the human being and the rest of the world. He begins by directing the reader to reflect on their own essence: the drop of fluid (nutfah) from which they originated, and indeed everything beyond that, once did not exist then came to be. This is known through necessary observation. He then asserts that all things are like you—they are bodies occupying space, contingent in existence and non-existence, and characterized by specific quantities and qualities. Therefore, just as you needed a determiner (mukhaṣṣiṣ) to specify your particular form and existence, so too must everything else need a determiner. The basis is the principle of the equality of two likes (mithlān) in all judgments—what is necessary, possible, and impossible. Since it is established for your essence that non-existence preceded it, the same must hold for the similar world. If it were possible for some part of the world to be eternal (qadīm), then one of two likes would be distinguished by a necessary attribute (eternity) that the other lacks, which is impossible because it would entail that the two are both alike and not alike—a contradiction. So through self-reflection and the recognition of likeness between you and all contingent beings, the decisive proof emerges that the entire world is created: its heights and depths, its Throne and Kursī, its principle and branch. Moreover, this shows that everything is incapable of creating itself or anything else, and is in need of a free-acting agent (fāʿil mukhtār), just as you are. The passage then provides a summary (ḥāṣiluhu): once it is established with necessity that the 'additional part' (al-zāʾid min al-dhāt)—i.e., the accidents and composite aspects beyond the original drop—are originated, and that natural natures (ṭabāʾiʿ) have no effect in producing the essence, and that the agent of the essence is free-acting, the author now turns to use that additional part as the basis for inferring the origination of the drop itself and the whole world. The inference is based on the realization of likeness between that additional part (which includes bodies and accidents) and the entire world. Since both are composed of bodies and accidents, they are alike, and likes must be equal in their modal status. The additional part is decisively originated (since it is clearly dependent and changing), so the entire world must be originated. If the world were partly eternal and partly originated, it would be different in what is necessary, which violates the principle of equality. To ward off a potential objection, the author explains why eternity can only be necessary for the eternal, not possible: if eternity were merely possible (jāʾiz) for the eternal, then non-existence would also be possible for it, requiring a determiner to specify existence over non-existence—but that would contradict the very definition of eternity (being necessary existence without beginning). Hence, eternal things must be necessary, and since the world is not necessary, it cannot be eternal. This whole argument is a standard Ashʿarī proof for the createdness of the universe.

Key Terms
نطفة (nutfah)
تماثل (tamāthul)
مخصص (mukhaṣṣiṣ)
حدوث (ḥudūth)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the analogy fails because the world is not literally like a human body.
Correction: The analogy relies on the common property of being a contingent body occupying space, not on all attributes. Both are 'jirm ya'muru farāghan' (body occupying space), which is sufficient for the principle of equality.

Checkpoint
What logical principle does Sanusi use to argue that the world must be created like the human?
A) Everything that exists has a cause.
B) Two likes must be equal in what is necessary, possible, and impossible.
C) The world is composed of atoms and accidents.
D) Human beings are the measure of all things.

Key Takeaway
The world's creation is proven by analogy with your own contingent existence, grounded in the principle that two likes share the same modal status.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that you and the entire universe are contingent—that you could have never existed—what would change about how you live today? Be specific about one action you would start or stop.
?
The passage says you were once a drop and then existed. How does that fact, when you really meditate on it, affect the weight you give to your current anxieties and ambitions? Are they as significant as you feel?
?
Sanusi argues that because you are contingent, the world must also have a determiner. If that determiner is Allah, what does that demand from you right now in terms of submission and gratitude? Write down one concrete way you will respond today.

The Impossibility of Partial Eternity
Sanusi argues against the possibility of partial eternity in the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the world cannot be partly eternal and partly created, and why eternity must be a necessary attribute.

Core Idea
If the world had both eternal and created parts, two likes would differ—a logical contradiction.

Sanusi now forestalls a potential objection: what if only some of the world is eternal? He shows that this is impossible because it would violate the principle of equality of likes. He also explains why eternity is a necessary attribute for anything eternal, using a reductio argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the conditional argument
Read from 'if it were possible for some of the world to be eternal' to 'that is impossible'.
Notice the structure: a supposition leading to an absurdity.
2
Understand why distinguishing one like by eternity is contradictory
If one of two likes had a necessary attribute (eternity) that the other lacked, it would be both a like and not a like—a contradiction.
3
Grasp the subsidiary proof that eternity is necessary
Sanusi asserts that eternity (qidam) is only necessary for the eternal. He hints at a proof: if eternity were possible, the eternal could have non-existence, requiring a determiner, which contradicts eternity.
This proof is expanded later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhān) for the creation (ḥudūth) of the entire universe. The author, Sanusi, constructs an argument by analogy (tamāthul) between the human being and the rest of the world. He begins by directing the reader to reflect on their own essence: the drop of fluid (nutfah) from which they originated, and indeed everything beyond that, once did not exist then came to be. This is known through necessary observation. He then asserts that all things are like you—they are bodies occupying space, contingent in existence and non-existence, and characterized by specific quantities and qualities. Therefore, just as you needed a determiner (mukhaṣṣiṣ) to specify your particular form and existence, so too must everything else need a determiner. The basis is the principle of the equality of two likes (mithlān) in all judgments—what is necessary, possible, and impossible. Since it is established for your essence that non-existence preceded it, the same must hold for the similar world. If it were possible for some part of the world to be eternal (qadīm), then one of two likes would be distinguished by a necessary attribute (eternity) that the other lacks, which is impossible because it would entail that the two are both alike and not alike—a contradiction. So through self-reflection and the recognition of likeness between you and all contingent beings, the decisive proof emerges that the entire world is created: its heights and depths, its Throne and Kursī, its principle and branch. Moreover, this shows that everything is incapable of creating itself or anything else, and is in need of a free-acting agent (fāʿil mukhtār), just as you are. The passage then provides a summary (ḥāṣiluhu): once it is established with necessity that the 'additional part' (al-zāʾid min al-dhāt)—i.e., the accidents and composite aspects beyond the original drop—are originated, and that natural natures (ṭabāʾiʿ) have no effect in producing the essence, and that the agent of the essence is free-acting, the author now turns to use that additional part as the basis for inferring the origination of the drop itself and the whole world. The inference is based on the realization of likeness between that additional part (which includes bodies and accidents) and the entire world. Since both are composed of bodies and accidents, they are alike, and likes must be equal in their modal status. The additional part is decisively originated (since it is clearly dependent and changing), so the entire world must be originated. If the world were partly eternal and partly originated, it would be different in what is necessary, which violates the principle of equality. To ward off a potential objection, the author explains why eternity can only be necessary for the eternal, not possible: if eternity were merely possible (jāʾiz) for the eternal, then non-existence would also be possible for it, requiring a determiner to specify existence over non-existence—but that would contradict the very definition of eternity (being necessary existence without beginning). Hence, eternal things must be necessary, and since the world is not necessary, it cannot be eternal. This whole argument is a standard Ashʿarī proof for the createdness of the universe.

Key Terms
قديم (qadīm)
قدِم (qidam)
تماثل (tamāthul)
محال (muḥāl)
برهان قاطع (burhān qāṭiʿ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What contradiction does Sanusi identify if some of the world were eternal?
A) The eternal part would be self-caused.
B) One of two likes would be distinguished by a necessary attribute, making it both a like and not a like.
C) The created part would be more perfect than the eternal part.
D) The world would have no beginning.

Key Takeaway
The world cannot be partly eternal because it would violate the logical principle that two likes must share the same necessity, possibility, or impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the entire world—including your very existence—is created and utterly dependent on Allah, what does that mean for the things you cling to as though they are self-sufficient or permanent? Are there parts of your life where you functionally treat creation as eternal?
?
The proof shows that any attribute of eternity in creation is impossible. How does this challenge your tendency to rely on worldly constructs like wealth, health, or relationships as if they are stable and lasting?
?
Everything glorifies His praise; nothing is independent. In what specific area of your life are you still acting as if you or something else has even a shred of self-sufficiency? What would it cost you to fully surrender that illusion today?

The Summary: How the Additional Part Proves Creation
Sanusi summarizes the inference from the additional part to the creation of the whole world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the summary of Sanusi's proof: the originated aspects of your body (the 'additional part') serve as a basis to prove the creation of the entire universe by likeness.

Core Idea
The additional part of your essence, being clearly created, shows that everything like it must be created.

Sanusi now summarizes the entire argument. He recounts what has been established—the additional part is originated, natural natures have no effect, and the agent is free-acting. Then he shows how this additional part, being composed of bodies and accidents just like the rest of the world, proves the creation of everything through the principle of equality of likes.

Guided Path
1
Review the established premises
Read the summary and note the three things that have been established: the additional part is originated, natural natures have no effect, and the agent of the essence is free-acting.
These premises are from earlier in the text.
2
Understand the inference from the additional part to the whole world
Since the additional part (bodies and accidents) is originated, and the entire world is like it (also bodies and accidents), the world must be originated by the principle of equality.
This is the core of the proof.
3
Grasp the proof of eternity's necessity
Sanusi explains that eternity is only necessary for the eternal; if it were possible, the eternal would need a determiner, which is incoherent.
This reinforces the argument against partial eternity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومِنْ هنا أيضاً تعلمُ : أنَّ تلك النطفة وسائر العالم لم یكنْ ثم كانَ ؛ إذْ كلُّهُ مثلُكَ ؛ جِرْمٌ يعمرُ فراغاً ، يمكنُ وجودُهُ وعدمه ، واتصافه بما هو عليهِ مِنَ المقادير والصفاتِ المخصوصة وبغيرها ، فيحتاج كما احتجت إلى مُخصص يُخصِّصُهُ بما هو عليهِ ؛ لوجوب استواء المثلين في كل ما يجبُ ويجوز ويستحيلُ ، وقد وجبَ لذاتِكَ سبقُ العدم، فكذلك يجبُ لسائر العالم المماثل لكَ ؛ إذ لو جاز أنْ يكونَ بعضُ العالم قديماً ، والقِدَمُ لا يكون إلا واجباً للقديم ؛ لِمَا يأتي.. لَلَزِمَ أنْ يختص أحدُ المِثْلين عن مِثْلِهِ بصفة واجبةٍ ، وهو محالٌ ؛ لِمَا يلزمُ مِنِ اجتماعِ متنافيين وهو أن يكونَ مِثْلاً غيرَ مِثْل ! فخرج لكَ بالنظرِ في ذاتِكَ ، وانعقاد التماثل بينك وبينَ سائر الممكنات .. البرهان القاطع على حدوثِ العالم کله ؛ علوهِ وسفلِهِ ، عرشِهِ وكرسيه ، أصله وفرعِهِ ، وأنَّ جميعَهُ عاجز عن إيجادِ نفسِهِ وعن إيجادِ غيرِهِ كعجزِكَ ، وأَنَّ الجميع مفتقر إلى فاعل مختار كافتقارِكَ ، وإِنْ مِنْ شيء إلا يُسبِّحُ بحمدِهِ . حاصلُهُ : أَنَّهُ بعدما استبانَ حدوث الزائدِ مِنَ الذاتِ على النطفة بالضرورة ، وأنَّ النطفة ونحوَها ممَّا يُقدَّرُ مِنَ الطبائع لا أثر لها في شيءٍ مِنَ الذاتِ ، وأنَّ فاعل الذاتِ فاعل مختار . . انعطف هنا بالاستدلالِ بذلكَ الزائدِ مِنَ الذاتِ على حدوثِ تلك النطفة وسائر العالم ، وأنَّ احتياج الجميع إلى فاعل مختار على حد السواء ، ولا أثر لبعض منه في بعض ألبتة . ووجه الاستدلال : تحقُّقُ المماثلة بينَ هذا الزائدِ والعالم كلّهِ ؛ إذْ هذا الزائد أجرام مُتحيّزةً وأعراض قائمة بها ، وسائر العالم كذلكَ والمِثْلانِ يجب استواؤُهما فيما يجبُ ويجوزُ ويستحيل ، وقد وجبَ الحدوث لذلكَ الزائدِ قطعاً ، فكذا يجبُ لسائر العالم ؛ لمماثلتِهِ إيَّاهُ ؛ إذ لو اختلف العالمُ بأن يكونَ بعضُه قديماً وبعضُه حادثاً.. لكان مختلفاً فيما يجب . وبيانُ الملازمةِ : أَنَّ القِدمَ لا يكون إلا واجباً للقديم ، وبرهانه : ما يأتي ؛ مِنْ كونِ القِدَمِ لو كانَ جائزاً للقديم لجاز عليه سبقُ العدمِ ، فيحتاج إلى مُخصِّصِ يُخصِّصُهُ بالوجودِ بدلاً عن العدمِ المجوّز ، وهو نقيضُ القِدَمِ المفروض ، فيلزمُ أنْ يكونَ قديماً غير قديم ! وهو تهافت (۱) ، وهذا معنى قولِهِ : ( والقِدَمُ لا يكون إلا واجباً للقديم لِمَا يأتي ) ، فهو مُعترِضُ بينَ الشرط وجوابه لبيان تلازمهما .
And from here also you learn: that that drop of fluid (nutfah) and the rest of the world were not then came to be; since all of it is like you; a body occupying space, its existence and non-existence possible, and its being characterized by what it has of specific measures and attributes and others, so it needs just as you needed a determiner (mukhaṣṣiṣ) to specify it as it is; because of the necessity of the two likes (mithlān) being equal in everything necessary, possible, and impossible. And non-existence has been preceded for your essence, so likewise it must be for the rest of the world which is similar to you; because if it were possible for some of the world to be eternal (qadīm), and eternity (qidam) is not except necessary for the eternal, as will come, then it would necessitate that one of the two likes be distinguished from its like by a necessary attribute, and that is impossible because it entails the conjunction of two contradictories: that it be a like and not a like! So there emerges for you, by reflecting on your essence and the establishment of likeness (tamāthul) between you and the rest of contingent things, the decisive proof (al-burhān al-qāṭiʿ) for the creation (ḥudūth) of the entire world: its heights and depths, its Throne and Kursī, its principle and branch; and that all of it is incapable of bringing itself into existence and of bringing anything else into existence like your incapacity; and that all of it is in need of a free-acting agent (fāʿil mukhtār) like your need; and there is nothing except that it glorifies His praise. Its summary (ḥāṣiluhu): After it became clear with necessity that the additional part (al-zāʾid) of the essence relative to the drop (al-nutfa) is originated, and that the drop and the like of it from what is considered as natural natures have no effect in anything of the essence, and that the agent of the essence is a free-acting agent, he turns here to infer by means of that additional part of the essence to the origination of that drop and the rest of the world, and that the need of all for a free-acting agent is equal, and there is no effect of some of it in any other at all. And the aspect of inference is: the realization of likeness (al-mumāthala) between this additional part and the entire world; for this additional part is bodies occupying space and accidents inhering in them, and the rest of the world likewise; and the two likes must be equal in what is necessary, possible, and impossible; and origination (al-ḥudūth) has been necessitated for that additional part decisively, so likewise it must be for the rest of the world because of its likeness to it; for if the world differed such that some of it were eternal and some were originated, it would differ in what is necessary. And the explanation of the entailment (mulāzama): that eternity (al-qidam) is only necessary for the eternal, and its proof: what will come; because if eternity were possible for the eternal, then non-existence would be possible for it, so it would need a determiner to specify it with existence instead of the possible non-existence, and that contradicts the supposed eternity, so it would follow that it is eternal and not eternal! And that is incoherence (tahāfut). And this is the meaning of his statement: 'And eternity is only necessary for the eternal, as will come.' So it is an interruption between the condition and its answer to show their entailment.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhān) for the creation (ḥudūth) of the entire universe. The author, Sanusi, constructs an argument by analogy (tamāthul) between the human being and the rest of the world. He begins by directing the reader to reflect on their own essence: the drop of fluid (nutfah) from which they originated, and indeed everything beyond that, once did not exist then came to be. This is known through necessary observation. He then asserts that all things are like you—they are bodies occupying space, contingent in existence and non-existence, and characterized by specific quantities and qualities. Therefore, just as you needed a determiner (mukhaṣṣiṣ) to specify your particular form and existence, so too must everything else need a determiner. The basis is the principle of the equality of two likes (mithlān) in all judgments—what is necessary, possible, and impossible. Since it is established for your essence that non-existence preceded it, the same must hold for the similar world. If it were possible for some part of the world to be eternal (qadīm), then one of two likes would be distinguished by a necessary attribute (eternity) that the other lacks, which is impossible because it would entail that the two are both alike and not alike—a contradiction. So through self-reflection and the recognition of likeness between you and all contingent beings, the decisive proof emerges that the entire world is created: its heights and depths, its Throne and Kursī, its principle and branch. Moreover, this shows that everything is incapable of creating itself or anything else, and is in need of a free-acting agent (fāʿil mukhtār), just as you are. The passage then provides a summary (ḥāṣiluhu): once it is established with necessity that the 'additional part' (al-zāʾid min al-dhāt)—i.e., the accidents and composite aspects beyond the original drop—are originated, and that natural natures (ṭabāʾiʿ) have no effect in producing the essence, and that the agent of the essence is free-acting, the author now turns to use that additional part as the basis for inferring the origination of the drop itself and the whole world. The inference is based on the realization of likeness between that additional part (which includes bodies and accidents) and the entire world. Since both are composed of bodies and accidents, they are alike, and likes must be equal in their modal status. The additional part is decisively originated (since it is clearly dependent and changing), so the entire world must be originated. If the world were partly eternal and partly originated, it would be different in what is necessary, which violates the principle of equality. To ward off a potential objection, the author explains why eternity can only be necessary for the eternal, not possible: if eternity were merely possible (jāʾiz) for the eternal, then non-existence would also be possible for it, requiring a determiner to specify existence over non-existence—but that would contradict the very definition of eternity (being necessary existence without beginning). Hence, eternal things must be necessary, and since the world is not necessary, it cannot be eternal. This whole argument is a standard Ashʿarī proof for the createdness of the universe.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the 'additional part' refers to something extra added to the essence, not the accidents.
Correction: The additional part (zāʾid min al-dhāt) refers to the accidents and composite aspects that are not part of the original drop, such as bodily attributes.

The originating of the additional part (the accidents and composite aspects of your body) proves the creation of the entire universe through the principle of likeness.

Proof of the World's Temporality through Similarity
Sanusi summarizes the proof of the world's temporality using similarity and the need for a free agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the principle of similarity (tamāthul) is used to prove that the entire world is temporal.

Core Idea
Similarity forces equal judgments: if one thing is temporal, all similars must be temporal.

This lesson presents a key argument from Sanusi's al-Sanusiyya. After establishing that a part of the world—the added part (zāʾid)—is temporal, the author extends this to the whole world using the principle that two similars must have the same necessary, possible, and impossible attributes. This argument is a cornerstone of the kalam proof for the contingency of the world.

Guided Path
1
Context: Temporality of the added part
Read how Sanusi first establishes that the added part (zāʾid) from the essence is temporal by necessity, based on earlier reasoning about the sperm and the free agent.
The 'added part' refers to the bodily development beyond the sperm.
2
The similarity principle
Identify that the added part and the entire world are alike: both consist of bodies and accidents. Understand the rule that two similars must be equal in what is necessary, possible, and impossible.
This is a fundamental axiom in kalam.
3
Applying the principle
See how Sanusi applies this: since the added part is necessarily temporal, the whole world must be temporal. If not, there would be a difference in necessary judgments, which is impossible for similars.
4
The implication about eternity
Examine the closing reasoning: eternity is only necessary for the eternal; if it were merely possible, a specifier would be needed, leading to contradiction. This reinforces why temporality cannot be partial.
This part is an interjection clarifying the necessity of qidam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حاصلُهُ : أَنَّهُ بعدما استبانَ حدوث الزائدِ مِنَ الذاتِ على النطفة بالضرورة ، وأنَّ النطفة ونحوَها ممَّا يُقدَّرُ مِنَ الطبائع لا أثر لها في شيءٍ مِنَ الذاتِ ، وأنَّ فاعل الذاتِ فاعل مختار . . انعطف هنا بالاستدلالِ بذلكَ الزائدِ مِنَ الذاتِ على حدوثِ تلك النطفة وسائر العالم ، وأنَّ احتياج الجميع إلى فاعل مختار على حد السواء ، ولا أثر لبعض منه في بعض ألبتة. ووجه الاستدلال : تحقُّقُ المماثلة بينَ هذا الزائدِ والعالم كلّهِ ؛ إذْ هذا الزائد أجرام مُتحيّزةً وأعراض قائمة بها ، وسائر العالم كذلكَ والمِثْلانِ يجب استواؤُهما فيما يجبُ ويجوزُ ويستحيل ، وقد وجبَ الحدوث لذلكَ الزائدِ قطعاً ، فكذا يجبُ لسائر العالم ؛ لمماثلتِهِ إيَّاهُ ؛ إذ لو اختلف العالمُ بأن يكونَ بعضُه قديماً وبعضُه حادثاً.. لكان مختلفاً فيما يجب . وبيانُ الملازمةِ : أَنَّ القِدمَ لا يكون إلا واجباً للقديم ، وبرهانه : ما يأتي ؛ مِنْ كونِ القِدَمِ لو كانَ جائزاً للقديم لجاز عليه سبقُ العدمِ ، فيحتاج إلى مُخصِّصِ يُخصِّصُهُ بالوجودِ بدلاً عن العدمِ المجوّز ، وهو نقيضُ القِدَمِ المفروض ، فيلزمُ أنْ يكونَ قديماً غير قديم ! وهو تهافت (۱) ، وهذا معنى قولِهِ : ( والقِدَمُ لا يكون إلا واجباً للقديم لِمَا يأتي ) ، فهو مُعترضُ بينَ الشرط وجوابه لبيان تلازمهما (۱) سيأتي ( ص ٢٣٩ ) .
Its summary: that after it became evident that the added thing from the essence is temporal with respect to the sperm necessarily, and that the sperm and its like from what is estimated from the natures have no effect in anything of the essence, and that the agent of the essence is a free agent... he turned here to argue by that added thing from the essence for the temporality of that sperm and the rest of the world, and that the need of all for a free agent is equal, and there is no effect of one part upon another at all. And the face of the proof is: the verification of similarity between this added thing and the entire world; for this added thing is composed bodies and accidents subsisting in them, and the rest of the world is likewise. And the two similars must be equal in what is necessary, possible, and impossible. And temporality has been established as necessary for that added thing absolutely, so likewise it is necessary for the rest of the world, due to its similarity to it; for if the world differed such that some of it is eternal and some temporal, it would differ in what is necessary. And the explanation of the implication: that eternity is only necessary for the eternal, and its proof is what follows: from the fact that if eternity were possible for the eternal, then prior non-existence would be possible for it, so it would need a specifier to specify it for existence instead of the possible non-existence, which is the opposite of the assumed eternity, so it would entail that it is eternal not eternal! It is a contradiction. And this is the meaning of his statement: 'And eternity is only necessary for the eternal, for what follows' – so it is an intervening between the condition and its answer to explain their entailment. (1) It will come (p. 239).
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage is a summary of a proof for the temporality of the world. The author begins by stating that after it was established that the added part (zāʾid) from the essence (dhāt) is temporal (ḥādith) relative to the sperm (nuṭfa) by necessity, and that the sperm and similar things from the natures (ṭabāʿiʿ) have no effect on the essence, and that the agent of the essence is a free agent (fāʿil mukhtār), he then turns to argue from that added part to the temporality of the sperm and the entire world. The argument is based on similarity (tamāthul): the added part and the whole world are alike in being composed of bodies (ajrām) and accidents (aʿrāḍ). Two similars (mithlān) must be equal in what is necessary, possible, and impossible. Since the added part is necessarily temporal, the whole world must also be temporal; otherwise, if some parts were eternal and some temporal, they would differ in what is necessary. The author then explains the implication (mulāzama): eternity (qidam) can only be necessary (wājib) for the eternal, not merely possible. If it were possible, the eternal could have been preceded by non-existence, requiring a specifier (mukhassis) to bring it into existence, which contradicts the assumption of eternity. This would entail that something is both eternal and not eternal — a contradiction (tahāfut). The author ends by noting that this explanation is the meaning of the phrase 'eternity is only necessary for the eternal' and it serves as an interjection between the condition and its answer.

Key Terms
المماثلة
الحدوث
القدم
الفاعل المختار
المثلان
الزائد من الذات
النطفة
الصفات النفسية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Similarity requires identical judgments in every respect, including accidental or relational properties.
Correction: The author specifies that similars must be equal only in what is necessary, possible, and impossible – not in all attributes. This is a technical point in kalam.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, why must the entire world be temporal if one part is temporal?
A) Because the whole world is composed of the same substance.
B) Because all parts of the world are similar, and similars must be equal in necessary judgments.
C) Because eternity is impossible for anything created.
D) Because the free agent chooses to create everything at once.

Key Takeaway
The similarity argument proves the temporality of the entire world by equating the necessary judgment of one part to all similar parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The principle of similarity demands equality in judgment. In your own life, do you hold yourself to the same standard you apply to others? Think of one area where you might be inconsistent. Will you repent from that inconsistency and seek fairness as God commands?
?
This proof shows that everything in this world is temporal and dependent on a free agent. What is one thing you are clinging to as if it will last forever—your health, wealth, or relationships? How can you reorient your heart to rely solely on the Eternal?
?
If the entire world is temporal and in need of a Creator, then your very existence is a gift renewed every moment. Are you living with a sense of urgent gratitude and accountability? What would change today if you truly believed you have no inherent permanence?

Allah's Existence
beginner
5 min
Introduction and Distinction of the Second Proof
Sanusi introduces a second proof for the world's temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof for the world's temporality based on change of attributes and how it differs from the first proof.

Core Idea
Change in attributes proves the world is created.

Sanusi presents a second argument for the world's creation. Unlike the first proof which focused on human essence, this proof considers all the world's attributes collectively. You will see how the change of attributes—both in potential and actuality—points to their createdness, and consequently to the createdness of the world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the distinction from the first proof.
2
Identify the main premise
The passage states the principle: 'Everything that changes is originated.' Find where this is stated.
3
Understand the difference from the first proof
Compare the scope: the first proof looked at a part (human), this proof looks at the whole.
This shows the author's methodological progression.

Source Text

تحقيق ( كلُّ متغيّر حادث )
وأيضاً : لو نظرت إلى تغير صفاتِ العالم قبولاً وحصولاً .. لدلَّكَ ذلك على حدوثها ؛ لِمَا يأتي من استحالة تغيرِ القديم ، ودلك حدوثها على حدوثِ موصوفها ؛ لاستحالة عروه عنها
هذا دليل آخر على حدوثِ العالم ، والفرقُ بينَهُ وبينَ الأَوَّلِ : أَنَّ المستدل في هاذا لم يخص نظرَهُ ببعض العالم دونَ بعض ، بل نظر في جميعِهِ نظراً واحداً وبوجه واحدٍ ، وفي الأَوَّلِ : نظرَ في بعضِهِ ؛ وهو ذاتُ الإنسانِ ، حتى إذا حصل العلم بحدوثها ضرورةً ودلته على وجودِ فاعل مختار ليسَ ذاتَهُ ولا جزءاً منها .. انعطف على سائر العالم فأثبت حدوثه بحدوثها ؛ لتحقق المماثلة بينهما ، وحقَّقَ أَنَّ صانعَهُ لا يمكنُ أنْ يكونَ ذاتَهُ ولا شيئاً مِنَ العالم ، فيئسَ في جميع الأمورِ مِنْ نفسِهِ ومِنْ جملة العالم لنفسِهِ ولغيره (۱) ، وانصرف النظرُ كَلُّهُ إِلَى مَنْ ليس كمثله شيء ، الغني عن كلِّ شيءٍ ، المفتقر إليه جميع ما سواه جل وعلا
·
وتقرير الدليلِ الذي أشار إليهِ هنا : أن تقول : العالم كلُّه صفاته (۱) أي : من نفع نفسه لنفسه ولغيرها ، وأيس من نفع بقية العالم لنفسها ولغيرها حامدي » ( ص ١٢٧ )
٢١٧
حادثة ، وكلُّ مَنْ صفاتُهُ حادثة فهو حادث ؛ فينتج : العالم حادث .
كله
أما كون صفات العالم حادثة : فدليله : أنَّها متغيرةٌ مِنْ وجود إلى عدم ، ومِنْ عدم إلى وجود قبولاً وحصولاً ، وكلُّ ما كان كذلك فهو حادث ؛ فينتج : صفات العالم حادثة .
ودليلُ التغيُّر : المشاهدةُ في بعضها ؛ كالحركات والأصوات ونحوها ؛ فإنَّها تُشاهَدُ طارئة بعد عدم ، ومعدومة بعد طروع ، والقبول فيما لا يُشاهَدُ فيهِ التغيرُ ؛ كسكون الأرض والألوان ونحوِ ذلك ؛ فإنَّ الأرضَ يجوز أن تتحرك وينعدم سكونها كما جاز ذلك فيما ماثلها مِنْ مُتحرِّك الأجرام ، وذا اللون المخصوص مثلاً يجوز أن ينعدم لونه ويتصف بغيرِهِ مِنَ الألوان كما اتصف بهِ مماثلُهُ مِنَ الجواهر والجواهر كلُّها متماثلة ، فيستحيل أن يجوز في بعضها ما لا يجوزُ في الآخرِ مِنْ حيثُ ذاتُهُ
فاستبانَ أنَّ صفاتِ العالم كلها تتغيَّرُ ؛ إما بالحصولِ أو بالقبولِ وهذا مِنْ غيرِ التفات إلى دليل استحالة بقاء الأعراض ، أما إذا التفتنا إليه فصفات العالم حينَئِذٍ كلُّها تتغيَّرُ بالحصول لا بالقبول إلى العدمِ وإلى الوجودِ تغيراً واجباً . وأما كونُ التغيرِ يستلزم الحدوثَ : فدليلُهُ : أَنَّ التغير مطلقاً يستحيلُ على القديم ؛ لأنَّهُ إِنْ كَانَ مِنْ عدمٍ إلى وجودِ كان وجوده طارئاً بعد عدم ، وهو عين الحدوثِ ، وقد فُرِضَ قديماً ، هذا خُلْفٌ ، وإنْ كانَ مِنْ وجود إلى عدم كان وجوده جائزاً ؛ بدليل قبولِهِ
Verification: Everything that changes is originated.
And also: If you were to consider the change of the world's attributes in terms of potentiality and actuality, that would indicate to you their temporality, due to what follows from the impossibility of change upon the eternal, and that (their temporality) indicates the temporality of their possessor, due to the impossibility of it being free of them.
This is another proof for the temporality of the world, and the difference between it and the first is that the arguer in this one did not restrict his consideration to some of the world without others, but rather considered all of it with one consideration and in one manner. In the first, he considered some of it, namely the essence of man, until knowledge of its temporality was obtained necessarily and led him to the existence of a free agent that is not his essence nor a part of it. Then he turned to the rest of the world and affirmed its temporality by its temporality, due to the verification of similarity between them. And he verified that its maker cannot be its essence nor anything from the world, so he despaired in all matters from himself and from the entirety of the world for himself and for others (1), and all attention turned to Him of whom there is nothing like, the Self-sufficient from everything, and all else is in need of Him, Exalted and High.
·
And the exposition of the proof he alludes to here is: that you say: The entire world, its attributes are originated, and everything whose attributes are originated is itself originated; thus the conclusion: the entire world is originated.
As for the fact that the world's attributes are originated: its proof is that they change from existence to non-existence and from non-existence to existence in terms of potentiality and actuality, and everything that is like that is originated; thus the conclusion: the world's attributes are originated.
And the proof of change: observation in some of them, like motions and sounds and the like, for they are observed occurring after non-existence and non-existent after occurrence. And potentiality (is observed) in that in which change is not observed, like the stillness of the earth and colors and the like. For the earth may move and its stillness become non-existent, just as that is possible in what resembles it among moving bodies. And this particular color, for example, may become non-existent and be characterized by another color, just as its similar among substances is characterized by it. And all substances are similar, so it is impossible that what is possible in some of them is not possible in another in terms of its essence.
Thus it became clear that all the world's attributes change, either by actuality or potentiality, and this without considering the proof of the impossibility of the survival of accidents. But if we consider it, then the world's attributes all change necessarily by actuality, not potentiality, to non-existence and to existence as a necessary change. And as for the fact that change entails temporality: its proof is that change absolutely is impossible for the eternal. Because if it is from non-existence to existence, its existence would be occurring after non-existence, which is the very essence of temporality, and it was assumed eternal; this is a contradiction. And if it is from existence to non-existence, its existence would be contingent, due to the proof of its acceptance of change.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is a presentation and detailed exposition of a second proof for the temporality of the world (ḥudūth al-ʿālam). The author begins by stating the principle: 'Everything that changes is originated.' He then introduces the proof: if you consider the change of the world's attributes in terms of potentiality (qabūl) and actuality (ḥuṣūl), you will conclude that they are originated (ḥāditha), because change is impossible for the eternal (qadīm). And if the attributes are originated, then the possessor of those attributes (the world itself) must also be originated, because it cannot be free of them.

The author distinguishes this second proof from the first proof (which focused on the human essence). In the first proof, the arguer looked at a part of the world (the human essence) and concluded its temporality necessarily, then extended that to the rest of the world by analogy. In this second proof, the arguer looks at all the world's attributes together in one unified consideration.

He then gives a formal exposition (taqrīr) of the proof in syllogistic form. The major premise: every being whose attributes are originated is itself originated. The minor premise: the world's attributes are originated. The conclusion: the world is originated.

To prove the minor premise, he argues that the world's attributes change from existence to non-existence and vice versa, both in actuality and potentiality. Everything that changes is originated. Therefore, the attributes are originated.

The evidence for change is twofold: for some attributes (like motion and sound), change is observed directly (mushāhada). For attributes where change is not directly observed (like the stillness of the earth or colors), potentiality is inferred: these attributes can change because similar substances have been observed to change. Since all substances are similar in essence, what is possible for one is possible for another.

He then notes that this argument does not rely on the proof of the impossibility of the survival of accidents (istiḥālat baqāʾ al-aʿrāḍ). If one does rely on that proof, then all attributes change necessarily by actuality, not just by potentiality.

Finally, he proves that change entails temporality: change is impossible for the eternal. If change is from non-existence to existence, then the thing becomes existent after non-existence, which is exactly temporality, contradicting eternity. If change is from existence to non-existence, then the existence of the thing is contingent (jāʾiz), which also negates necessary eternal existence.

Key Terms
متغيّر
حادث
قديم
صفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the first and second proofs are identical in method.
Correction: The author explains the difference: the first proof focuses on a part (human essence) and then extends by analogy, while the second proof considers all attributes uniformly.

Checkpoint
What is the main difference between the first and second proofs for the world's temporality according to this passage?
A) The first proof uses observation, the second uses potentiality.
B) The first proof considers a part (human), the second considers all attributes collectively.
C) The first proof proves the existence of God, the second proves the world's temporality.
D) The first proof is based on change, the second on contingency.

Key Takeaway
The second proof establishes the temporality of the world by observing the change of all its attributes, without restricting the analysis to a part.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You experience change every moment—your mood, health, thoughts, even your faith. If you really believed that every change in you is a sign that you are not in control of your own existence, would you still cling to the illusion of self-sufficiency? What would it look like today to surrender your changing self to the One who never changes?
?
Your attributes—intelligence, strength, reputation—are temporary gifts. Is there an area of your life where you act as if they are permanent? How will you remind yourself today that even these are created and will pass, so that your trust is placed only in the Eternal?

Syllogistic Form and Evidence for Change
Sanusi presents the formal syllogism and evidence for attribute change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the formal syllogism of the second proof and the evidence for the change of the world's attributes.

Core Idea
All world's attributes change, so they are created.

Sanusi now presents the proof in a structured syllogism. He argues that the world's attributes are all originated because they change—either by actual observation or by potentiality. You will learn how he uses both observation and inference to establish the minor premise.

Guided Path
1
Follow the syllogism
Identify the major and minor premises and the conclusion.
The major premise: everything whose attributes are originated is itself originated. The minor premise: the world's attributes are originated.
2
Examine the evidence for change
Note how he uses observation for some attributes and potentiality for others.
This shows depth of reasoning.
3
Consider the role of similarity of substances
Understand why what is possible for one substance is possible for all.
This is key to the argument.

Commentary
This passage is a presentation and detailed exposition of a second proof for the temporality of the world (ḥudūth al-ʿālam). The author begins by stating the principle: 'Everything that changes is originated.' He then introduces the proof: if you consider the change of the world's attributes in terms of potentiality (qabūl) and actuality (ḥuṣūl), you will conclude that they are originated (ḥāditha), because change is impossible for the eternal (qadīm). And if the attributes are originated, then the possessor of those attributes (the world itself) must also be originated, because it cannot be free of them.

The author distinguishes this second proof from the first proof (which focused on the human essence). In the first proof, the arguer looked at a part of the world (the human essence) and concluded its temporality necessarily, then extended that to the rest of the world by analogy. In this second proof, the arguer looks at all the world's attributes together in one unified consideration.

He then gives a formal exposition (taqrīr) of the proof in syllogistic form. The major premise: every being whose attributes are originated is itself originated. The minor premise: the world's attributes are originated. The conclusion: the world is originated.

To prove the minor premise, he argues that the world's attributes change from existence to non-existence and vice versa, both in actuality and potentiality. Everything that changes is originated. Therefore, the attributes are originated.

The evidence for change is twofold: for some attributes (like motion and sound), change is observed directly (mushāhada). For attributes where change is not directly observed (like the stillness of the earth or colors), potentiality is inferred: these attributes can change because similar substances have been observed to change. Since all substances are similar in essence, what is possible for one is possible for another.

He then notes that this argument does not rely on the proof of the impossibility of the survival of accidents (istiḥālat baqāʾ al-aʿrāḍ). If one does rely on that proof, then all attributes change necessarily by actuality, not just by potentiality.

Finally, he proves that change entails temporality: change is impossible for the eternal. If change is from non-existence to existence, then the thing becomes existent after non-existence, which is exactly temporality, contradicting eternity. If change is from existence to non-existence, then the existence of the thing is contingent (jāʾiz), which also negates necessary eternal existence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that only observed change counts as evidence.
Correction: Sanusi uses both observation for some attributes and potentiality for others based on similarity of substances.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why can we infer that the stillness of the earth can change?
A) Because stillness is an accident that cannot survive.
B) Because similar moving bodies have been observed to change.
C) Because all substances are eternal.
D) Because the earth is composed of atoms.

Key Takeaway
The world's attributes change either actually or potentially, proving they are created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that every attribute you possess—your strength, your health, your very life—is either actually changing or capable of change. If you truly believed this, which of your daily habits would change? Will you make a conscious effort today to thank Allah for each breath, knowing it could be your last?
?
Sanusi argues that because all substances are similar, what is possible for one is possible for all. How does this principle confront your pride in your own stability or achievements? What would it mean to you if you realized that your perceived 'fixed' character traits are not permanent? How can you actively seek tazkiyah (purification) knowing that change is always possible?
?
The proof does not rely on the impossibility of accidents surviving. Yet, do you live as if your good deeds and bad deeds are permanently recorded and unchangeable? How does this lesson give you hope for repentance and motivation to change your ways immediately?

Proof that Change Entails Temporality
Sanusi proves that change is impossible for the eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why change is impossible for the eternal and how this proves the temporality of the world.

Core Idea
Change and eternity cannot coexist.

Sanusi finalizes the argument by showing that change necessarily implies temporality. He proves that if something changes, it cannot be eternal. You will see two cases: change from non-existence to existence, and change from existence to non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the impossibility of change for the eternal
Read the proof carefully.
2
Examine the two scenarios
Notice the two cases: from non-existence to existence, and from existence to non-existence.
Each leads to contradiction with eternity.
3
Connect to the broader argument
How does this complete the proof for the world's temporality?

وأيضاً : لو نظرت إلى تغير صفاتِ العالم قبولاً وحصولاً .. لدلَّكَ ذلك على حدوثها ؛ لِمَا يأتي من استحالة تغيرِ القديم ، ودلك حدوثها على حدوثِ موصوفها ؛ لاستحالة عروه عنها
هذا دليل آخر على حدوثِ العالم ، والفرقُ بينَهُ وبينَ الأَوَّلِ : أَنَّ المستدل في هاذا لم يخص نظرَهُ ببعض العالم دونَ بعض ، بل نظر في جميعِهِ نظراً واحداً وبوجه واحدٍ ، وفي الأَوَّلِ : نظرَ في بعضِهِ ؛ وهو ذاتُ الإنسانِ ، حتى إذا حصل العلم بحدوثها ضرورةً ودلته على وجودِ فاعل مختار ليسَ ذاتَهُ ولا جزءاً منها .. انعطف على سائر العالم فأثبت حدوثه بحدوثها ؛ لتحقق المماثلة بينهما ، وحقَّقَ أَنَّ صانعَهُ لا يمكنُ أنْ يكونَ ذاتَهُ ولا شيئاً مِنَ العالم ، فيئسَ في جميع الأمورِ مِنْ نفسِهِ ومِنْ جملة العالم لنفسِهِ ولغيره (۱) ، وانصرف النظرُ كَلُّهُ إِلَى مَنْ ليس كمثله شيء ، الغني عن كلِّ شيءٍ ، المفتقر إليه جميع ما سواه جل وعلا
·
وتقرير الدليلِ الذي أشار إليهِ هنا : أن تقول : العالم كلُّه صفاته (۱) أي : من نفع نفسه لنفسه ولغيرها ، وأيس من نفع بقية العالم لنفسها ولغيرها حامدي » ( ص ١٢٧ )
٢١٧
حادثة ، وكلُّ مَنْ صفاتُهُ حادثة فهو حادث ؛ فينتج : العالم حادث .
كله
أما كون صفات العالم حادثة : فدليله : أنَّها متغيرةٌ مِنْ وجود إلى عدم ، ومِنْ عدم إلى وجود قبولاً وحصولاً ، وكلُّ ما كان كذلك فهو حادث ؛ فينتج : صفات العالم حادثة .
ودليلُ التغيُّر : المشاهدةُ في بعضها ؛ كالحركات والأصوات ونحوها ؛ فإنَّها تُشاهَدُ طارئة بعد عدم ، ومعدومة بعد طروع ، والقبول فيما لا يُشاهَدُ فيهِ التغيرُ ؛ كسكون الأرض والألوان ونحوِ ذلك ؛ فإنَّ الأرضَ يجوز أن تتحرك وينعدم سكونها كما جاز ذلك فيما ماثلها مِنْ مُتحرِّك الأجرام ، وذا اللون المخصوص مثلاً يجوز أن ينعدم لونه ويتصف بغيرِهِ مِنَ الألوان كما اتصف بهِ مماثلُهُ مِنَ الجواهر والجواهر كلُّها متماثلة ، فيستحيل أن يجوز في بعضها ما لا يجوزُ في الآخرِ مِنْ حيثُ ذاتُهُ
فاستبانَ أنَّ صفاتِ العالم كلها تتغيَّرُ ؛ إما بالحصولِ أو بالقبولِ وهذا مِنْ غيرِ التفات إلى دليل استحالة بقاء الأعراض ، أما إذا التفتنا إليه فصفات العالم حينَئِذٍ كلُّها تتغيَّرُ بالحصول لا بالقبول إلى العدمِ وإلى الوجودِ تغيراً واجباً . وأما كونُ التغيرِ يستلزم الحدوثَ : فدليلُهُ : أَنَّ التغير مطلقاً يستحيلُ على القديم ؛ لأنَّهُ إِنْ كَانَ مِنْ عدمٍ إلى وجودِ كان وجوده طارئاً بعد عدم ، وهو عين الحدوثِ ، وقد فُرِضَ قديماً ، هذا خُلْفٌ ، وإنْ كانَ مِنْ وجود إلى عدم كان وجوده جائزاً ؛ بدليل قبولِهِ
Verification: Everything that changes is originated.
And also: If you were to consider the change of the world's attributes in terms of potentiality and actuality, that would indicate to you their temporality, due to what follows from the impossibility of change upon the eternal, and that (their temporality) indicates the temporality of their possessor, due to the impossibility of it being free of them.
This is another proof for the temporality of the world, and the difference between it and the first is that the arguer in this one did not restrict his consideration to some of the world without others, but rather considered all of it with one consideration and in one manner. In the first, he considered some of it, namely the essence of man, until knowledge of its temporality was obtained necessarily and led him to the existence of a free agent that is not his essence nor a part of it. Then he turned to the rest of the world and affirmed its temporality by its temporality, due to the verification of similarity between them. And he verified that its maker cannot be its essence nor anything from the world, so he despaired in all matters from himself and from the entirety of the world for himself and for others (1), and all attention turned to Him of whom there is nothing like, the Self-sufficient from everything, and all else is in need of Him, Exalted and High.
·
And the exposition of the proof he alludes to here is: that you say: The entire world, its attributes are originated, and everything whose attributes are originated is itself originated; thus the conclusion: the entire world is originated.
As for the fact that the world's attributes are originated: its proof is that they change from existence to non-existence and from non-existence to existence in terms of potentiality and actuality, and everything that is like that is originated; thus the conclusion: the world's attributes are originated.
And the proof of change: observation in some of them, like motions and sounds and the like, for they are observed occurring after non-existence and non-existent after occurrence. And potentiality (is observed) in that in which change is not observed, like the stillness of the earth and colors and the like. For the earth may move and its stillness become non-existent, just as that is possible in what resembles it among moving bodies. And this particular color, for example, may become non-existent and be characterized by another color, just as its similar among substances is characterized by it. And all substances are similar, so it is impossible that what is possible in some of them is not possible in another in terms of its essence.
Thus it became clear that all the world's attributes change, either by actuality or potentiality, and this without considering the proof of the impossibility of the survival of accidents. But if we consider it, then the world's attributes all change necessarily by actuality, not potentiality, to non-existence and to existence as a necessary change. And as for the fact that change entails temporality: its proof is that change absolutely is impossible for the eternal. Because if it is from non-existence to existence, its existence would be occurring after non-existence, which is the very essence of temporality, and it was assumed eternal; this is a contradiction. And if it is from existence to non-existence, its existence would be contingent, due to the proof of its acceptance of change.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is a presentation and detailed exposition of a second proof for the temporality of the world (ḥudūth al-ʿālam). The author begins by stating the principle: 'Everything that changes is originated.' He then introduces the proof: if you consider the change of the world's attributes in terms of potentiality (qabūl) and actuality (ḥuṣūl), you will conclude that they are originated (ḥāditha), because change is impossible for the eternal (qadīm). And if the attributes are originated, then the possessor of those attributes (the world itself) must also be originated, because it cannot be free of them.

The author distinguishes this second proof from the first proof (which focused on the human essence). In the first proof, the arguer looked at a part of the world (the human essence) and concluded its temporality necessarily, then extended that to the rest of the world by analogy. In this second proof, the arguer looks at all the world's attributes together in one unified consideration.

He then gives a formal exposition (taqrīr) of the proof in syllogistic form. The major premise: every being whose attributes are originated is itself originated. The minor premise: the world's attributes are originated. The conclusion: the world is originated.

To prove the minor premise, he argues that the world's attributes change from existence to non-existence and vice versa, both in actuality and potentiality. Everything that changes is originated. Therefore, the attributes are originated.

The evidence for change is twofold: for some attributes (like motion and sound), change is observed directly (mushāhada). For attributes where change is not directly observed (like the stillness of the earth or colors), potentiality is inferred: these attributes can change because similar substances have been observed to change. Since all substances are similar in essence, what is possible for one is possible for another.

He then notes that this argument does not rely on the proof of the impossibility of the survival of accidents (istiḥālat baqāʾ al-aʿrāḍ). If one does rely on that proof, then all attributes change necessarily by actuality, not just by potentiality.

Finally, he proves that change entails temporality: change is impossible for the eternal. If change is from non-existence to existence, then the thing becomes existent after non-existence, which is exactly temporality, contradicting eternity. If change is from existence to non-existence, then the existence of the thing is contingent (jāʾiz), which also negates necessary eternal existence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that change could occur in an eternal being if its essence remains.
Correction: Sanusi shows that change from non-existence to existence is temporality itself, and change from existence to non-existence entails contingency, both incompatible with eternity.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what does change from existence to non-existence imply about the being?
A) It is eternal.
B) Its existence is contingent.
C) It is necessary.
D) It cannot change.

Key Takeaway
Change in any form negates eternity; therefore, the changing world must be originated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You change every day—your mood, your health, your thoughts. If change proves temporality, what does that mean for your own existence? Are you living as if you are temporary, or do you cling to worldly things as if they will last forever?
?
Sanusi shows that anything that can change cannot be eternal. Your entire life is a series of changes. Does this truth sink into your heart, making you turn towards the Eternal, or do you still act as if you are independent and self-sufficient?
?
The proof demonstrates that change implies contingency—you are contingent. Have you truly submitted to the Necessary Being, Allah, or do you live as if you are the master of your own destiny? What one practical step can you take today to acknowledge your dependence on Him?
